  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_cbe6a1d4-3c2b-4d96-a9d8-40e7a5620460'

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer918512024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15004804486593069313">I finished this and thought it was very good, maybe quite simply because Marie Curie had such an interesting life, rather than the author's writing skills. Fascinating and moving. Science details are explained just enough so you understand.....at least most of the time. Sometimes books go on and on and you drown in the details, but not here. You learn about her childhood, her devotion to science, her love for her children and husband and science. I repeated science twice, and that was done on pu</span>
  <span id="freeText15004804486593069313" style="display:none">I finished this and thought it was very good, maybe quite simply because Marie Curie had such an interesting life, rather than the author's writing skills. Fascinating and moving. Science details are explained just enough so you understand.....at least most of the time. Sometimes books go on and on and you drown in the details, but not here. You learn about her childhood, her devotion to science, her love for her children and husband and science. I repeated science twice, and that was done on purpose. I want to keep this short, but you also get relevant details on WW1 and Polish history too. Marie Curie had an interesting life. As a person she is someone to admire, but neither are her failures shied from. I do recommend the book. Fine narration of the audiobook by Eliza Foss.<br /><br />Maybe I should also listen to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/49416459.The_Half_Life_of_Marie_Curie" title="The Half-Life of Marie Curie by Lauren Gunderson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Half-Life of Marie Curie</a>!</span>

<div id="review_1849552484" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1849552484" />
    <a title="Jean" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/23100707-jean"><img alt="Jean" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562348872p2/23100707.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1849552484?book_show_action=true">Dec 31, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jean" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jean" href="/user/show/23100707-jean">Jean</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=audio-book">audio-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=world-war-1">world-war-1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=european-history">european-history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=women-s-rights">women-s-rights</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/23100707-jean?shelf=science">science</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1849552484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11042185111859471887">There have been so many biographies about Marie Curie (Marya Salomea Sklodowska 1867-1934) that any new book is going to either present new material or look at the information from a different viewpoint. Goldsmith, a social historian, has chosen to pursue “the real woman”. Curie was one of only two women to graduate from the Sorbonne with a science degree. Curie was born in Russian occupied Poland and the University of Warsaw did not allow women to attend. She married Pierre Curie and shortened </span>
  <span id="freeText11042185111859471887" style="display:none">There have been so many biographies about Marie Curie (Marya Salomea Sklodowska 1867-1934) that any new book is going to either present new material or look at the information from a different viewpoint. Goldsmith, a social historian, has chosen to pursue “the real woman”. Curie was one of only two women to graduate from the Sorbonne with a science degree. Curie was born in Russian occupied Poland and the University of Warsaw did not allow women to attend. She married Pierre Curie and shortened her name.<br /><br />Goldsmith covers primarily the hatred, bigotry and prejudice Curie had to overcome rather than on her scientific discoveries. Goldsmith’s weakness is her difficulty in attempting to explain the scientific and theoretical aspects of Marie Curie’s work. Instead Goldsmith tells how the scientific establishment detested her. She won the Nobel Prize in 1903 for Physics. She shared this with her husband Pierre for discovering radioactivity. She was not allowed to give the keynote lecture that the winner traditionally gives because she was a woman. In 1911 Curie, now a widow, won a second Nobel Prize this time in Chemistry for the discovery of Radium. She won this one alone. Curie, a winner of two Nobel Prizes, was refused membership in the French Academy of Science because she was a woman. During WWI, she designed a mobile x-ray machine and then trained her daughter in its use. Her daughter then trained technicians to use it. In 1934 her daughter, Irene, discovered artificial radioactivity and won the Nobel Prize. Marie Curie discovered polonium, radium and radioactivity. She died on 3 July 1934 of aplastic pernicious anemia caused by radium radiation.<br /><br />The book was well written and researched. The weakness is noted above. The book was interesting, but there are more in-depth biographies about Marie Curie available.<br /><br />Eliza Foss does a good job narrating the book. Foss is a stage actor and award winning audiobook narrator. I have listened to numerous books she has narrated.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11042185111859471887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2236168261" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2236168261" />
    <a title="Jenna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/82453-jenna"><img alt="Jenna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601135180p2/82453.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2236168261?book_show_action=true">Jan 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenna" href="/user/show/82453-jenna">Jenna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/82453-jenna?shelf=memoir-and-biography">memoir-and-biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2236168261" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13054875555894169111">I bought this book five years ago after stumbling on this <a href='http://www.bookslut.com/features/2011_06_017729.php' rel='nofollow noopener'><i>Bookslut</i> review</a>, which introduced me to the fascinating facts of Marie Curie's youthful romance with the Polish mathematician Casimir Zorawski, a story that so captivated me that I ultimately wrote <a href='http://waccamawjournal.com/poetry/i-casimir-zorawski-a-mathematician-of-poland/' rel='nofollow noopener'>this poem</a> about it. For five years, I left all but the first four chapters of the book unread (the Zorawski episode and its aftermath are wrapped up in chapters 3-4). Then, this long weekend, I decided that now was as good a time as any to fini</span>
  <span id="freeText13054875555894169111" style="display:none">I bought this book five years ago after stumbling on this <a href="http://www.bookslut.com/features/2011_06_017729.php" rel="nofollow noopener"><i>Bookslut</i> review</a>, which introduced me to the fascinating facts of Marie Curie's youthful romance with the Polish mathematician Casimir Zorawski, a story that so captivated me that I ultimately wrote <a href="http://waccamawjournal.com/poetry/i-casimir-zorawski-a-mathematician-of-poland/" rel="nofollow noopener">this poem</a> about it. For five years, I left all but the first four chapters of the book unread (the Zorawski episode and its aftermath are wrapped up in chapters 3-4). Then, this long weekend, I decided that now was as good a time as any to finish it.<br /><br />Though much of the lore surrounding the beginnings of the fields of radiology and nuclear medicine was already known to me from my medical education and professional training, this book still had much to teach me: for example, I hadn't previously been aware that Marie Curie was intimate friends with Hertha Ayrton, the inspiration for the character of Mirah in George Eliot's <i>Daniel Deronda</i> (and a strong scientist and feminist in her own right). Nor had I known just what a great marriage Marie and Pierre Curie had, with the rock-solid Pierre stalwartly supporting his wife through her recurrent episodes of depression in a way that reminded me of the partnership of Leonard and Virginia Woolf. Also surprising to me was the horrible way Marie Curie's reputation was very nearly annihilated by the scandal arising from her affair with married scientist Paul Langevin after Pierre's death (Curie was pressured to leave France when the affair became public knowledge, and a member of the Nobel committee even wrote in a letter that the scandal made the committee think twice about awarding Curie the 1911 Nobel Prize in Chemistry!). Finally, I was pleasantly surprised to learn about the Curies' involvement in the discovery of sonar (Pierre had done some research on piezoelectric quartz crystals before turning his attention to the study of radioactivity, and Marie supplied Langevin with some of this quartz to facilitate his pioneering research into sonar), about Marie and daughter Irene's role in establishing an all-female corps of radiology technologists to serve on the battlefield in World War I, and about the whole Curie family's firm stances against war and brutality throughout their lives. While some scientists of the time made dishonorable concessions to the rise of Nazism, fascism, and antisemitism, the Curie family's reputation remains pure.<br /><br />The book also does a good job of evoking what the process of doing science is actually like, how it relies on identifying unanswered questions, designing elegant experiments that answer those questions, painstakingly carrying out those experiments in a tidy replicable way, and, finally, interpreting the experimental results correctly -- Goldsmith gives several good examples of situations where even great minds like the Curies incorrectly interpreted experimental results, leading to confusion and temporary delays in scientific advancement.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13054875555894169111" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_57865817" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/57865817" />
    <a title="Brent M.  Jones" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2084201-brent-m-jones"><img alt="Brent M.  Jones" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598544084p2/2084201.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/57865817?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Brent M.  Jones" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Brent M.  Jones" href="/user/show/2084201-brent-m-jones">Brent M.  Jones</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2084201-brent-m-jones?shelf=2000-2015-read">2000-2015-read</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer57865817" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5504544801282725000">The title "Obsessive Genius" refers to many different sides of Marie Curie's life. Some may have considered her story to be somewhat of a feminist message but the title describes the "person", not just the woman behind the research and the life that went with it. <br/><br/>Marva Salomee Sklodowska, Marie Curie, was born in Poland. She went to Paris and got her degrees at the Sorbonne and then spent most of her life in France. Her singular passion was for her work but that changed when she met Pierre Curie</span>
  <span id="freeText5504544801282725000" style="display:none">The title "Obsessive Genius" refers to many different sides of Marie Curie's life. Some may have considered her story to be somewhat of a feminist message but the title describes the "person", not just the woman behind the research and the life that went with it. <br /><br />Marva Salomee Sklodowska, Marie Curie, was born in Poland. She went to Paris and got her degrees at the Sorbonne and then spent most of her life in France. Her singular passion was for her work but that changed when she met Pierre Curie. Her obsessive passion for work, studies, research, and her husband, were complex and present a very interesting woman. Their first Nobel Prize in 1903 was a mutual effort but her second came later in her life and was clearly something that she could not be denied. She was denied the opportunity to co accept the first award and sit in the audience. She had done much if not most of the work. <br /><br />Like the book Einstein, by Walter Isaacson, this book lets you see a life through the lens of a particular science. In both cases you learn about both the person and the science. This type of biography lets you see the historical events you thought you knew all about very differently through the lens of a particular person and the science that fills their life. The book is well done and well worth reading. More on this book at web site. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.connectedeventsmatter.com">www.connectedeventsmatter.com</a></span>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7993750076660576393">Previous to reading this book, I knew only the basics regarding Marie Curie. What a strong woman she was, trying to balance home, children and her work which was all consuming. Needless to say, her health suffered, even before the radium.   <span id="freeText7993750076660576393" style="display:none">Previous to reading this book, I knew only the basics regarding Marie Curie. What a strong woman she was, trying to balance home, children and her work which was all consuming. Needless to say, her health suffered, even before the radium. I was very surprised with how wide spread they used radium, putting it in face cream, inhalers for vigor, and even in drinks. Of course they didn't realize the risks, but it really made an impression on my how many dangerous things skipped under the radar before regulation and the Food and Drug administration.<br /><br />I thought maybe I would get bogged down with all the chemistry and physics involved, but the author gave us a clear and concise explanation, one that was not too difficult to follow. Also thought she did a very good job, mixing their health, their home and their work. All in all a very good read about the lives of this famous woman. So admired her spunk, because she basically had to fight every step of the way the prejudices of the time.</span>

<div id="review_363100450" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/363100450" />
    <a title="Mike" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/309250-mike"><img alt="Mike" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1338575393p2/309250.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/363100450?book_show_action=true">Jul 07, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mike" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mike" href="/user/show/309250-mike">Mike</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer363100450" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1636864412766464881">It was the Fourth of July, and I needed a book to read; what more appropriate material than the biography of a Polish expatriate living and working in France?<br/><br/>All explanations of why I read it aside, I think this book is a great example of the top-down non-fiction book. It is a straightforward retelling of Marie Curie's life, and--as long as you only view it as such--it works. Unfortunately there isn't much more to the book, and it suffers some attributes common to the top-down style of writing, </span>
  <span id="freeText1636864412766464881" style="display:none">It was the Fourth of July, and I needed a book to read; what more appropriate material than the biography of a Polish expatriate living and working in France?<br /><br />All explanations of why I read it aside, I think this book is a great example of the top-down non-fiction book. It is a straightforward retelling of Marie Curie's life, and--as long as you only view it as such--it works. Unfortunately there isn't much more to the book, and it suffers some attributes common to the top-down style of writing, chiefly that the story sounds great in short (one of the greatest scientists of the late eighteenth/early nineteenth centuries fights to overcome poor funding and gender bias in the academy to make some of the greatest contributions to modern physics!), but the end result feels sort of mailed in, as though Goldsmith only chose to write about Curie because it was easier than writing about Mary Wollstonecraft or Abigail Adams. As such, Curie comes off as two dimensional martyr for historical currents that happen to interest Goldsmith (She was a genius whose contributions helped change the world--but look at the price she paid!). I wondered what stories there were at the margins of the book, which interesting little bits Goldsmith had cut in order to meet a word limit.<br /><br />Bottom line: if you've got a boring paper to write, this is a great source. If you want to understand a great mind, a time and place, a different world, this might be a good starting point.<br /><br />*A bottom up book would be something that starts from a small question or observation (or at least purports to start from something small) and then stumbles across Big Themes, rather than starting with the Themes and looking for examples that support it. A good example of a bottom-up book would be Born to Run or Moneyball.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1636864412766464881" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_242317194" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Elizabeth" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7098916-elizabeth"><img alt="Elizabeth" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476189273p2/7098916.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/242317194?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elizabeth" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elizabeth" href="/user/show/7098916-elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7098916-elizabeth?shelf=biography">biography</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer242317194" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3788737337540411965">I really enjoyed this book and learned so much not only about Madame Curie but also about the scientific world of the 19th and early 20th century. <br/><br/>Marie Curie is most famous for identifying and isolating the element radium. She and her husband Pierre worked obsessively on this great project for a few years until their experiments were finally successful. <br/><br/>The extensive exposure to radium eventually killed both Marie and her husband Pierre. Marie lived much longer than Pierre but her health was al</span>
  <span id="freeText3788737337540411965" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book and learned so much not only about Madame Curie but also about the scientific world of the 19th and early 20th century. <br /><br />Marie Curie is most famous for identifying and isolating the element radium. She and her husband Pierre worked obsessively on this great project for a few years until their experiments were finally successful. <br /><br />The extensive exposure to radium eventually killed both Marie and her husband Pierre. Marie lived much longer than Pierre but her health was always on the decline. In fact, the Curie clothing and papers are still to this day radioactive!<br /><br />Marie Curie won two Nobel Prizes in her life and was the first and only woman Nobel Prize recipient until her daughter Irene won the prize.<br /><br />Marie and Pierre had two daughters and one of her daughters, Irene, also became a famous scientist. And Irene's daughter also became a famous scientist!<br /><br />Marie's story is a classic example of a person overcoming all odds to do great things. She overcame the disadvantage of growing up in an occupied country. She overcame great poverty. And she overcame being a woman in the field of science in the 19th and early 20th century when women were not valued for their scientific minds.<br /><br />She was born in a country that did not exist. That country was Poland. She lived in Warsaw which was owned by Russia. Her father was a Polish patriot and he raised his children to be fierce Polish patriots like him. In fact, Marie named one of the elements that she discovered, Polium, after her beloved Poland.<br /><br />Marie was a very intelligent child who resented the Russian run schools she attended. Though her father was a professor, the family was impoverished because the Russians only wanted Russian teachers in the schools in Poland. Thus, her father struggled to provide for his family which included several children.<br /><br />Marie's mother died of tuberculosis when she was a child. Perhaps, because of this, Marie was emotionally distant the rest of her life even when she herself was a mother.<br /><br />Marie suffered from life long depression and had a few nervous breakdowns throughout her life. She suffered greatly after her husband died and even blamed herself for his death.<br /><br />One of the most poignant quotations in the book came from a letter Marie wrote to a friend. After her husband's death, she began to withdraw from her friends and family even more so than she normally did. Here is what she said in her own words about that terrible time:<br /><br />"I have completely lost the habit of conversations without a set goal."<br /><br />Anyway, I learned A LOT from this book and I highly recommend it. It is very easy to read and understand even if you are like me and don't know much about chemistry and physics. It is written for the layman and it is only about 250 pages. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3788737337540411965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kathleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/15893444-kathleen"><img alt="Kathleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1375203965p2/15893444.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1435754959?book_show_action=true">Nov 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kathleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kathleen" href="/user/show/15893444-kathleen">Kathleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/15893444-kathleen?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1435754959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9462556588458726714">Marie Curie is a name that I've been familiar with for most of my life, but I didn't know much more than that she was a French scientist. Marie Curie was a Polish woman who lived in France and was the first woman to win a Nobel prize, and she won it twice. She was a physicist and chemist and studied radioactivity.<br/><br/>Marie Curie (1867-1934) was born in a world that did not acknowledge woman as scholars. She fought for every opportunity to study and was also hampered by poverty. But she prevailed. <br/><br/>Wh</span>
  <span id="freeText9462556588458726714" style="display:none">Marie Curie is a name that I've been familiar with for most of my life, but I didn't know much more than that she was a French scientist. Marie Curie was a Polish woman who lived in France and was the first woman to win a Nobel prize, and she won it twice. She was a physicist and chemist and studied radioactivity.<br /><br />Marie Curie (1867-1934) was born in a world that did not acknowledge woman as scholars. She fought for every opportunity to study and was also hampered by poverty. But she prevailed. <br /><br />While it was enlightening to read about her hard won success in science, it was fascinating to read about her life as an almost motherless child and then as a mother herself. She was kept at a distance from her mother who had tuberculosis and Curie felt the loss of that love. However, when she became a mother, she was so obsessed with her research that she did not mother her own children very much. Maybe she really didn't know how. Goldsmith portrays her as a physically and emotionally weak woman - she gave everything to her scientific work.<br /><br />I'm definitely not a science person and I did get lost in all the scientific details and in the many names of other scientists and their territorial squabbles. I was more interested in Curie's personal life. Obsessive Genius is a fair introduction to a fascinating woman.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9462556588458726714" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Lina " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5332942-lina"><img alt="Lina " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1578603196p2/5332942.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lina " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lina " href="/user/show/5332942-lina">Lina </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5332942-lina?shelf=science-y">science-y</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer305350949" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17226548190653742217">I was very surprised about this book. I wanted to read it for a book club, and while it sounded interesting, I didn't expect too much, because I don't like most biographies.<br/><br/>This one was a great one, though! I kept turning page after page because the story really drew me in.<br/><br/>Particularly memorable: the irony in it all, when Marie and Pierre, in a race to discover more about the future medical breakthrough (polonium and radium), purify more and more of the radioactive material that would destroy bo</span>
  <span id="freeText17226548190653742217" style="display:none">I was very surprised about this book. I wanted to read it for a book club, and while it sounded interesting, I didn't expect too much, because I don't like most biographies.<br /><br />This one was a great one, though! I kept turning page after page because the story really drew me in.<br /><br />Particularly memorable: the irony in it all, when Marie and Pierre, in a race to discover more about the future medical breakthrough (polonium and radium), purify more and more of the radioactive material that would destroy both of their healths.<br /><br />Phew... I feel like I just got off a roller coaster ride. It's a great book, not too long, but definitely captivating!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17226548190653742217" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1267828119" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1267828119" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/893768-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1395247653p2/893768.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1267828119?book_show_action=true">Apr 29, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/893768-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780393051377" href="/book/show/255506.Obsessive_Genius">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/893768-barbara?shelf=nobel-prize-winners">nobel-prize-winners</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1267828119" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8795331581559791373">I really enjoyed this book. For me, it had just the right amount of science in it--enough so that I could understand what Curie was doing and why her work was important, but not so much that my educational defects got in the way. I am SO ignorant about technical things, having spent my life focused on music, literature, history, and art.<br/><br/>I was inspired by Marie's early dedication to learning and her desire to help her sister obtain a university education. It was also appealing to read about her l</span>
  <span id="freeText8795331581559791373" style="display:none">I really enjoyed this book. For me, it had just the right amount of science in it--enough so that I could understand what Curie was doing and why her work was important, but not so much that my educational defects got in the way. I am SO ignorant about technical things, having spent my life focused on music, literature, history, and art.<br /><br />I was inspired by Marie's early dedication to learning and her desire to help her sister obtain a university education. It was also appealing to read about her loving relationship with her husband.<br /><br />It was shocking, though not surprising, to read how her contributions were ignored by the Nobel committee. Even after they grudgingly agreed to include her in the 1903 prize, they wouldn't let her speak or sit on the dais with the other winners, and the prize money for the three winners (the Curies and Henri Becquerel) was divided in two rather than three parts, as though Marie and Pierre were only one person. <br /><br />Later, when she had her affair with colleague Paul Langevin, the prevailing double standard made HER the villain in the story, although Langevin was the one cheating on his wife. Apparently he was not treated as a pariah but she was. Both professionally and personally, Marie had to struggle against the sexist attitudes of her time. (And have things really changed so much, even now??)<br /><br />I found it interesting to read about Pierre's fatal accident and realize that I know exactly where it had happened...have been at the exact spot many times. It was very easy to visualize. Horrible way for Pierre to die, and terrible how she found out about it. I felt very sorry for her and especially for the children, who were never allowed to talk about their father and to grieve properly.<br /><br />And speaking of her children....Marie certainly was not a warm and welcoming parent. The book showed her as very focused on her work to the near-total exclusion of her children and her social life. I suppose that is what an "obsessive genius" must be like. Had she been a more ordinary parent, she probably wouldn't have had the time and energy to accomplish what she did professionally.<br /><br />I hadn't known about the role she played during World War I. It was fascinating to read about the mobile X-ray units she created and operated. <br /><br />I'm happy that her adopted country finally recognized her wartime and scientific contributions and gave her a place in the previously all-male Pantheon.<br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="8795331581559791373" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_10937112" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/10937112" />
    <a title="yoli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/173637-yoli"><img alt="yoli" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/10937112?book_show_action=true">Dec 23, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="yoli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="yoli" href="/user/show/173637-yoli">yoli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780393051377" href="/book/show/255506.Obsessive_Genius">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">feminists and scientists</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/173637-yoli?shelf=2007">2007</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer10937112" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4240084928391218573">Overall I found it a pretty well written book. More pictures would’ve been nice, because I must admit to being a rather superficial person who likes to “put a face to the story.” There are a few snapshots but they are scattered throughout the biography and probably number less than 10. That is truly depressing for the visual learners. You’re better off just looking at the cover jacket to remind yourself what Curie looked liked.<br/><br/>It was interesting how Goldsmith did not just write about Curie eithe</span>
  <span id="freeText4240084928391218573" style="display:none">Overall I found it a pretty well written book. More pictures would’ve been nice, because I must admit to being a rather superficial person who likes to “put a face to the story.” There are a few snapshots but they are scattered throughout the biography and probably number less than 10. That is truly depressing for the visual learners. You’re better off just looking at the cover jacket to remind yourself what Curie looked liked.<br /><br />It was interesting how Goldsmith did not just write about Curie either. Obviously the other characters were related, in some way, to her, but their stories were told as well. We learned what sort of man Pierre was, and how their relationship went along. We learned what it was like to have the Marie Curie for a mother, and how it influenced her two daughters in two different ways. Relying heavily on interviews with her granddaughter and facts gleaned from the Curie papers, professional and personal, it presents a pretty full-bodied portrait of the famous scientist. There is also some commentary from Goldsmith, towards the end, about what drew her to Marie Curie in the first place. This was interesting because, well, it’s a valid question! Also explains a lot about how she formatted the book and why she wrote it the way she did.<br /><br />It did not ring as terribly biased; even the French government which is criticized must accept that criticism isn’t unwarranted. Knowing that the book was written primarily from words Curie herself had written in letters and diaries also helped ground the book more. I feel that it is more credible because it got its facts from the woman herself. Of course, that isn’t to say Goldsmith could not have “read” the works in a certain light. But at least the works themselves were true. Her addressing the “myth of Madame Curie,” mostly self-fabricated for financial reasons, was also important. It’s hard to understand a woman who does not want to be understood or scrutinized. Her death probably makes it easier. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4240084928391218573" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText46174317444112056" style="display:none">I've finished this wonderful biography. I can't believe it took me so long to finally pick it up. Thanks for reading it with me Greta - you are the reason I finally did!<br /><br />I think the final quote from Marie sums it up wonderfully:<br /><br />"I am among those who think that science has great beauty. They meant as equals of the mind, and because of that, their love had such an enormously sturdy base in which to grow. <br /><br />I do feel for their children. It happens often when two people love each more than the moon and stars for there to be enough space for their offspring. It's impossible to compete with that sort of love. <br /></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12348339265245379716">The book started out on a positive note with the author bio at the start: Barbara Goldsmith was born in New York, graduated from Wellesley College...<br/> In elementary school we read biographies that promote the Marie Curie myth - her struggles in an cold lab until she succeeds at finding radium and becomes the first woman to win one, and then two Nobel prizes. This biography presents Marie Curie as she struggled not only against strong social pressure against women in science but against depression</span>
  <span id="freeText12348339265245379716" style="display:none">The book started out on a positive note with the author bio at the start: Barbara Goldsmith was born in New York, graduated from Wellesley College...<br /> In elementary school we read biographies that promote the Marie Curie myth - her struggles in an cold lab until she succeeds at finding radium and becomes the first woman to win one, and then two Nobel prizes. This biography presents Marie Curie as she struggled not only against strong social pressure against women in science but against depression as well. She was not an easy person to get along with, and her stubbornness seems to have played a role in her unwillingness to recognize the effects that radium was having on her body. <br /> From the intellectual history perspective, it's fascinating to read about the time when knowledge that the average high school student today takes for granted was still being debated.</span>

<div id="review_764296147" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/764296147" />
    <a title="Jamie Collins" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/321673-jamie-collins"><img alt="Jamie Collins" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450191045p2/321673.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/764296147?book_show_action=true">Nov 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jamie Collins" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jamie Collins" href="/user/show/321673-jamie-collins">Jamie Collins</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780393051377" href="/book/show/255506.Obsessive_Genius">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/321673-jamie-collins?shelf=history-and-biography">history-and-biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/321673-jamie-collins?shelf=science-and-medicine">science-and-medicine</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer764296147" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6903157508576156840">This is an easy read, about 230 pages, and serves as a good introduction to Curie and her work - which is described at a very high level, with no in-depth explanation of the science. The prose does feel a bit condensed sometimes, as if the author struggled to keep the book under a maximum word count, perhaps to fit the format of this “Great Discoveries” series.<br/><br/>I think I will look for more books on the scientists and mathematicians of the early 20th century. This series includes books on Feynman </span>
  <span id="freeText6903157508576156840" style="display:none">This is an easy read, about 230 pages, and serves as a good introduction to Curie and her work - which is described at a very high level, with no in-depth explanation of the science. The prose does feel a bit condensed sometimes, as if the author struggled to keep the book under a maximum word count, perhaps to fit the format of this “Great Discoveries” series.<br /><br />I think I will look for more books on the scientists and mathematicians of the early 20th century. This series includes books on Feynman (I’ve read some of his autobiographical works), Rutherford, Turing, Gödel, and Henrietta Leavitt.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_223663007" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

    <a title="Ihab Alsafeen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6670329-ihab-alsafeen"><img alt="Ihab Alsafeen" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223663007" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11410047495752924109">In reality this books talked about a genius woman who discovered uranium for medical healing and that thing started to make a good step for evolution in science. In general the part I liked it was when she insist to keep doing what she loved and keep forward for her dream and doesn't give up for this one. Even when men was looking down to women she showed up and she got support from her father and her sister to keep forward for her dreams. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_1882446377" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1882446377" />
    <a title="Rakesh M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4962158-rakesh-m"><img alt="Rakesh M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1301060541p2/4962158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1882446377?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rakesh M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rakesh M" href="/user/show/4962158-rakesh-m">Rakesh M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4962158-rakesh-m?shelf=non-fiction">non-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4962158-rakesh-m?shelf=biography">biography</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4962158-rakesh-m?shelf=library_reads">library_reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4962158-rakesh-m?shelf=great_discoveries_series">great_discoveries_series</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1882446377" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11840150593264764232">This biography not only records the lives of Madam Curie, Pierre Curie and her family, colleagues. It gives glimpses of the inner lives of the genius, and it also gives an account of the societal norms and scientific progress across Europe in the war ridden times of WWI and II. Although this book is a biography it seems like reading historical fiction novels. <br/><br/>Some lessons to remember :<br/>All fears must be conquered. <br/>Create your own rules. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('kNYVU8fnznGjsK3ESvW7wPV9loEt/Yc/waAcbmBtgSv6EvVMmIf7VcER7igZ7GMgZdObkyBfPRVEmVvsJh1gPg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('fYw6S9f96M4zRRogE7G7Rne8ZA4jPXpeKN970kb0hAIXSNpUiJ3d6lHkWcxAqGOm5xJpHC6fwHSt5jxQAIRlFw==')})
  }

the Oslo peace process, and her activism today.Leila Khaled\'s example gives unique insights into the Palestinian struggle through one remarkable life – from the tension between armed and political After the birth of her first child in 2006, Turkish writer Elif Shafek suffered from postpartum depression that triggered a profound personal crisis. Infused with guilt, anxiety, and bewilderment about whether she could ever be a good mother, Shafak stopped writing and lost her faith in words altogether. In this elegantly written memoir, she retraces her journey from free-spirited, nomadic artist to dedicated but emotionally wrought mother. Identifying a constantly bickering harem of women who live inside of her, each with her own characteristics--the cynical intellectual, the goal-oriented go-getter, the practical-rational, the spiritual, the maternal, and the lustful--she craves harmony, or at least a unifying identity. As she intersperses her own experience with the lives of prominent authors such as Sylvia Plath, Virginia Woolf, Alice Walker, Ayn Rand, and Zelda Fitzgerald, Shafak looks for a solution to the inherent conflict between artistic creation and responsible parenting.  With searing emotional honesty and an incisive examination of cultural mores within patriarchal societies, Shafak has rendered an important work about literature, motherhood, and spiritual <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer11065331408682427856">an American author, journalist, and philanthropist. She received critical and popular acclaim for her best selling books, essays, articles, and her philanthropic work. She was awarded four honoris causa doctorates, and numerous awards; been elected to the American Academy of Arts and Sciences, two Presidential Commissions, and the New York State Council on the Arts; and honored by The New York Pub</span>
  <span id="freeText11065331408682427856" style="display:none">an American author, journalist, and philanthropist. She received critical and popular acclaim for her best selling books, essays, articles, and her philanthropic work. She was awarded four honoris causa doctorates, and numerous awards; been elected to the American Academy of Arts and Sciences, two Presidential Commissions, and the New York State Council on the Arts; and honored by The New York Public Library Literary Lions as well as the Literacy Volunteers, the American Academy in Rome, The Authors Guild, and the Guild Hall Academy of Arts for Lifetime Achievement. In 2009, she received the Knight’s Cross of the Order of Merit medal from the Republic of Poland. In November 2008, Goldsmith was elected a “Living Landmark” by the New York Landmarks Conservancy. She has three children and six grandchildren. The Financial Times declared that "Goldsmith is leaving a legacy—one of art, literature, friends, family and philanthropy."</span>
